  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_932749a0-3273-48a4-a92e-a0c051209593'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5970864278328334907"><p>El día en que ETA anuncia el abandono de las armas, Bittori se dirige al cementerio para contarle a la tumba de su marido el Txato, asesinado por los terroristas, que ha decidido volver a la casa donde vivieron. ¿Podrá convivir con quienes la acosaron antes y después del atentado que trastocó su vida y la de su familia? ¿Podrá saber quién fue el encapuchado que un día lluv</p></span>
  <span id="freeText5970864278328334907" style="display:none"><p>El día en que ETA anuncia el abandono de las armas, Bittori se dirige al cementerio para contarle a la tumba de su marido el Txato, asesinado por los terroristas, que ha decidido volver a la casa donde vivieron. ¿Podrá convivir con quienes la acosaron antes y después del atentado que trastocó su vida y la de su familia? ¿Podrá saber quién fue el encapuchado que un día lluvioso mató a su marido, cuando volvía de su empresa de transportes? Por más que llegue a escondidas, la presencia de Bittori alterará la falsa tranquilidad del pueblo, sobre todo de su vecina Miren, amiga íntima en otro tiempo, y madre de Joxe Mari, un terrorista encarcelado y sospechoso de los peores temores de Bittori. ¿Qué pasó entre esas dos mujeres? ¿Qué ha envenenado la vida de sus hijos y sus maridos tan unidos en el pasado? Con sus desgarros disimulados y sus convicciones inquebrantables, con sus heridas y sus valentías, la historia incandescente de sus vidas antes y después del cráter que fue la muerte del Txato, nos habla de la imposibilidad de olvidar y de la necesidad de perdón en una comunidad rota por el fanatismo político.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="5970864278328334907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div id=buyButtonContainer class="uitext buttons buyableFeature u-marginTopXSmall u-paddingBottomXSmall u-bottomGrayBorder" data-book-id=31842429 >

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2226208795" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2226208795" />
    <a title="Kalliope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope"><img alt="Kalliope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2226208795?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=fiction-spanish">fiction-spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=21-century">21-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=20-century">20-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2226208795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10188479719050193204"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1515615422i/24865657._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This novel has been a huge success in Spain recently. From September 2016 to July 2017, that is ten months after it was first published, it has run through twenty editions. Several of my friends have read or are reading it and a few recommended it to me. Generally I am not much drawn to bestsellers and this one was no exception. But I received it recently as a present from a family member, so I decided to face my prejudices.<br/><br/>The novel deals with two Basque families from around the 1980s to the re</span>
  <span id="freeText10188479719050193204" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1515615422i/24865657._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This novel has been a huge success in Spain recently. From September 2016 to July 2017, that is ten months after it was first published, it has run through twenty editions. Several of my friends have read or are reading it and a few recommended it to me. Generally I am not much drawn to bestsellers and this one was no exception. But I received it recently as a present from a family member, so I decided to face my prejudices.<br><br>The novel deals with two Basque families from around the 1980s to the recent present and how they deal with the activity of the terrorist group ETA. It begins when the group announced it was suspending its armed fight (20th of October 2011), and finishes a few months later. The story, however, offers a great deal of flashbacks, so the reader really goes back three decades.<br><br>The two families were closed friends but during those years turned into enemies. ETA assassinated the father in one of the families, and one son of the other became a member of the terrorist group. Not until later in the novel do we learn whether this son was the actual killer of the man who had bought him toys and ice creams when he was a child. And in this the novel is successful, in portraying how the one killing affected the lives of the other eight; for it affected the family of the dead father and the family of the terrorist member.<br><br>Although the story is punctuated with real facts (names of victims and a few dates), the two families remain typological; the same applies to the small town where this trauma unfolds. We only know that it is one of small cities in the province of San Sebastian (<i>Donostia</i> for the Basques).<br><br>The novel has more than six hundred pages but I read it fast. It contains a great deal of dialogue, the chapters are short, and the plot moves from one character to another. The reader is easily captivated with this constant shift of viewpoints and narrative voices. There are a total of nine characters: one family has two kids while the other three. The assassinated man is also given his chapters and his own voice in several of the flashbacks.<br><br>The various voices, however, did not seem to me sufficiently differentiated from one person to another. They all swear similarly, which for the daughters when young did not seem to me credible. Inevitably, I kept hearing throughout the novel the ventriloquist voice of Aramburu.<br><br>One of the gripping aspects is that I found myself checking the exact date when any of the real assassinations are mentioned, and trying to remember where I was on that day. Three of them are clear in my mind. In one, the <i>Hipercor</i> bombing in Barcelona, on the 19th of June of 1987, I was passing by in a train with visiting American friends, on our way to Gerona. This was ETA’s worst attack with 21 dead and 45 injured. The victims were mostly women and children since <i>Hipercor</i> is a shopping mall and it was Friday afternoon.<br><br>In spite of feeling very engaged with the novel, I found it however unsatisfactory. In parts it acquires somewhat of a ‘feuilleton’, with easy plot or reader traps such as the homosexuality of one of the sons (to cater to all tastes), or adding extraneous melodrama when one of the characters suffers a severe ictus, etc.…The abundant swear words do not altogether disguise the sporadic sentimentality. The novel is too long, then, due to the unnecessary parts. There has also been some criticism by others in that Aramburu does not completely know his Basque roots and that his command of Basque is faulty; the quotes and the Basque names are supposedly not properly rendered. This I would not be able to detect.<br><br>Fernando Aramburu was born in San Sebastian in 1959, the year ETA was founded. He currently lives in Alemania where he moved in 1985, for sentimental reasons (he married a German woman) but also because, like many other Basques, he exiled himself.<br><br>In one of the later chapters we visit a formal gathering of the ‘victims’ (mostly the families of those assassinated) in which there are a series of speakers. One is an unnamed judge, who from his physical description, I think I know who it is (I once sat next to him in a concert), and the other is a writer who has just published a novel about the ETA terrorism. Of course the reader knows who this is, and the various paragraphs can be considered Aramburu’s imbedded manifesto. He explains why he wrote such a novel while also expressing, through the voice of one of the victims, that it is dubiously ethical to make a spectacle out of their suffering. Like in many other complex situations, there is always more than one side. What did not seem to me to be justified in the least, however, is the assertion that “ETA’s terrorism is not useful for fighting the Right. For that a civil war is better.” WHAT? Has Aramburu forgotten what a democratic system is? Does he share then some of ETA’s objectives? Is violence and killing the only way for one's political preferences to prevail? What about the ballot? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(my translation – <i>Además el terrorismo de ETA no sirve para atacar a la derecha. Para eso es mucho major la guerra civil’</i>) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>What comes across clearly in this novel, depressingly, is the overall cowardice of the population of these small cities during those decades of terror. The oppressive and claustrophobic atmosphere imposed by the terrorist organization grew in a constant crescendo, sucking people into their own cause or expelling them out of their hometown. More of this and less of the ‘feuilleton’ aspects would have made this work more powerful and convincing. <br><br>Recently, while in a bus in Madrid, I overheard two women talking about this book. One of them, obviously a Basque, said that she had no interest in reading the book because she knew what it had been like, knew several of the victims, had decided to leave the place and had no intention of reviving those years. She also expressed doubt on the authenticity of the treatment by writer.<br><br>The novel is being translated into several languages. Even if I have not given it five stars, I consider it still worthwhile reading if anyone is interested in learning more about this brutal organization and the suffering they have caused. They killed 829 people.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="10188479719050193204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2343699745" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2343699745" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3143117-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571421692p2/3143117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2343699745?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3143117-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788490663196" href="/book/show/32042873-patria">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=spanish-lit">spanish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=vasco">vasco</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=espa%C3%B1ol">español</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2343699745" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12955215741033140657">Two women. Bittori and Miren. They live in a small Basque town in northern Spain. One rainy day, Bittori’s husband Txato, owner of a trucking company, is gunned down by ETA terrorists. Why? He refused to pay money to ETA. He was not patriotic enough.<br/><br/>Miren’s son is charged in the killing. Spurred on by his mother patriotic fervor, her eldest son Joxe Mari grows to become part of ETA. And he is picked to kill his neighbor, a friend of the family.<br/><br/>The two families are torn apart in a land torn apart</span>
  <span id="freeText12955215741033140657" style="display:none">Two women. Bittori and Miren. They live in a small Basque town in northern Spain. One rainy day, Bittori’s husband Txato, owner of a trucking company, is gunned down by ETA terrorists. Why? He refused to pay money to ETA. He was not patriotic enough.<br /><br />Miren’s son is charged in the killing. Spurred on by his mother patriotic fervor, her eldest son Joxe Mari grows to become part of ETA. And he is picked to kill his neighbor, a friend of the family.<br /><br />The two families are torn apart in a land torn apart by ETA and their fight for their own homeland (Patria). In October 2011, ETA halted their armed struggle, where thousands had died over four decades. The story unravels just as ETA makes their announcement.<br /><br />Fernando Aramburu has written a truly remarkable book. The power of violence, hatred and suffering is countered by a believable story of survival, hope and love. This book challenged so many notions and delved into the psyche of both sides through the pain and suffering that happens to both families. <br /><br />Both families are challenged but then Arantxa, Miren’s daughter has a brain aneurism and is confined to a wheelchair. Pay back? Her other son, Gorka refuses the violence of his brother and spreads the Euskera (Basque language) through writing and radio. Even the children of Bittori are affected and cope with the loss of their father as best as they can. The women in this story are remarkable. Strong willed and determined, they move the story to its surprising conclusion.<br /><br />The bleak subject matter was at times depressing but as the 640 pages moved along, there was a change in the tone. They were moving past their tragic past. Using short chapters (125 in total) creates an illusionary sense of time. We move backwards/forwards while memories unfold into a narrative that unravels time itself. The present is made up of the past while pondering the future.<br /><br />I had read much about this book and it does live up to its reputation. Aramburu gives us enough to know about the Basque people and yet delivers a strong anti-war narrative. One needs to read this book. It says so much about all people caught in endless violence. Powerful and yet very human.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12955215741033140657" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2680496717" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2680496717" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2680496717?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=hispanophilia">hispanophilia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=novels-novellas-short-stories">novels-novellas-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=no-hablo-ingl%C3%A9s">no-hablo-inglés</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2680496717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11036898015867519086">I feel myself doubly unqualified to rate and review this book, which has recently become ubiquitous here in Spain. For one, I decided that I would use the audiobook to improve my listening ability; and even though I normally had no trouble on the level of words and sentences (the book’s style is quite simple), I often lost the main thread. Indeed, at times I was so confused that I feel embarrassed in even marking this book as ‘read.’ What is more, much of this book’s emotional impact seems to co</span>
  <span id="freeText11036898015867519086" style="display:none">I feel myself doubly unqualified to rate and review this book, which has recently become ubiquitous here in Spain. For one, I decided that I would use the audiobook to improve my listening ability; and even though I normally had no trouble on the level of words and sentences (the book’s style is quite simple), I often lost the main thread. Indeed, at times I was so confused that I feel embarrassed in even marking this book as ‘read.’ What is more, much of this book’s emotional impact seems to come from its ability to bring up memories of the troubled years when ETA was terrorizing the country—of which I have none. So I am not exactly in the best position to write an intelligent review.<br /><br />For whatever it is worth, then, I confess that I did not much like this book. On the level of structure, it is (to borrow Henry James’s phrase) loose and baggy—that is, much too long and too little focused. Something like 90% of the book is dialogue, and this dialogue often does not accomplish very much, either in terms of plot or character development. The plot is wandering, slow-moving, and at times aimless; while the characters are hardly distinguishable from one another. Each person talks using the same expressions and following the same cadences; and the narrative voice, too, becomes quite repetitive by the end. However, this monotony ended up making the book excellent listening practice, since there is so much redundant material, and the dialogue, however pointless, is very natural and idiomatic.<br /><br />But my main criticism of this book was that its genuinely interesting theme —how ETA affected Basque life during this time—is buried in a mass of unnecessary and distracting telenovela-type material. Cheating boyfriends, a serious illness, a gay son: cheap drama that serves as a sauce to cover up bland characters. This is a shame, for when Aramburu does come back to his main theme, he does an admirable job in representing the moral compromises and the climate of fear that characterize the Basque struggle for independence. Nevertheless, again I admit that, however flimsy the material, the telenovela aspects were easy reading and thus also served as good listening practice. And since I am not really in a position to judge this novel on literary grounds, I will end by recommending the audiobook to everyone hoping to improve their Spanish.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11036898015867519086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3368433552" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3368433552" />
    <a title="Zizeloni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18712852-zizeloni"><img alt="Zizeloni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428869432p2/18712852.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3368433552?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zizeloni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zizeloni" href="/user/show/18712852-zizeloni">Zizeloni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789601680965" href="/book/show/42944654">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3368433552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15415709953576084091">I am torn between 4 and 5 stars.<br/><br/>The book is Patria, by Fernando Aramburu. It deals with two families living in a Basque village. They are very close until they are separated by political reasons: one family's son is a member of ETA and the other family's father is murdered by ETA.<br/><br/>You have 9 narrators, small chapters that jump to different parts of the timeline with no pattern (at least not an obvious pattern I am too lazy to discover it). It sounds hectic, but it really isn't, I never got confus</span>
  <span id="freeText15415709953576084091" style="display:none">I am torn between 4 and 5 stars.<br /><br />The book is Patria, by Fernando Aramburu. It deals with two families living in a Basque village. They are very close until they are separated by political reasons: one family's son is a member of ETA and the other family's father is murdered by ETA.<br /><br />You have 9 narrators, small chapters that jump to different parts of the timeline with no pattern (at least not an obvious pattern I am too lazy to discover it). It sounds hectic, but it really isn't, I never got confused, after a couple of sentences I could understand which PoV we have and when it is in the timeline. And for me that was the most important and special feature of this book. The writer is really skillful.<br /><br />I liked the chapters from the children's PoV, except the ETA member, although these chapters had the most action, I found it boring and illogical. He never persuaded me why he did that. And even worse, his mother. The worst character in the book. I never understood her. And maybe that is what is missing. I didn't understand the ETA reasoning at all. Maybe the writer should have tried more to make me conflicted, making me think that maybe they had a point... Their actions were definitely wrong, but maybe I could learn more about their philosophy.<br /><br />I guess the writer assumes we know more on the politics of the situation than I do.<br /><br />You see 9 people, all suffering for different reasons. But the one suffering the most for me, was the daughter with the stroke. That was too difficult to think. And it makes you think, why create so many problems? There are already so many problems in this life...<br /><br />So the reason I am torn between 4 and 5 stars: I think as a writing style, as a work of literature, it is perfect. But while I think it is perfect, I don't love the book. But I guess this is a topic you can't love. Or maybe I couldn't connect with one character because there were so many PoVs and maybe the characters I like weren't even mentioned in some chapters. But it deserves 5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15415709953576084091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1772745474" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1772745474" />
    <a title="Anna Maria Ballester Bohn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2954563-anna-maria-ballester-bohn"><img alt="Anna Maria Ballester Bohn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1258495316p2/2954563.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1772745474?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Maria Ballester Bohn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Maria Ballester Bohn" href="/user/show/2954563-anna-maria-ballester-bohn">Anna Maria Ballester Bohn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1772745474" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2120525714578346273">Probably not the height of literary style or sophistication. And so so great precisely because of that. So necessary. So simple. So human. So profound, so full of compassion. And, last but not least, so horrifying and gripping. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2995775353" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2995775353" />
    <a title="Marija Assereckova" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4121751-marija-assereckova"><img alt="Marija Assereckova" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390766257p2/4121751.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2995775353?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marija Assereckova" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marija Assereckova" href="/user/show/4121751-marija-assereckova">Marija Assereckova</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781509858033" href="/book/show/44548777-homeland">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4121751-marija-assereckova?shelf=modern">modern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4121751-marija-assereckova?shelf=spain">spain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2995775353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1847145860092354184">It’s my first encounter with Aramburu and “Homeland” stands somewhere in between of strong three stars and far-fetched four. It’s definitely interesting to read this book, it’s not boring and written in a lively manner. However, in many ways, it’s inferior to other politically dense novels I’ve read.<br/><br/>First of all, Aramburu prefers to take the beaten track. There are ETA members, who are bad, and there are all others, politically more or less inert Basques, who are different but generally good; th</span>
  <span id="freeText1847145860092354184" style="display:none">It’s my first encounter with Aramburu and “Homeland” stands somewhere in between of strong three stars and far-fetched four. It’s definitely interesting to read this book, it’s not boring and written in a lively manner. However, in many ways, it’s inferior to other politically dense novels I’ve read.<br /><br />First of all, Aramburu prefers to take the beaten track. There are ETA members, who are bad, and there are all others, politically more or less inert Basques, who are different but generally good; this is proved by a total happy-end of all storylines. Secondly, there are too many characters in this book, and some of them don’t contribute to the story at all. It may be important to describe in detail all the members of the two principal families, but to dedicate a full chapter to the ETA member’s brother’s partner’s daughter? Besides, some storylines disturbingly resemble soap operas. It’s clear that the author wanted to give the full picture and to make the story inclusive, but characters can’t be just functional, such as “a gay son”.<br /><br />However, my main problem with this book isn’t the length or the whiff of a telenovela. It’s the fact that Aramburu seems to be banal. The main antagonist, the ETA fighter, is bluntly contrasted with the “right Basque nationalism” – the terrorist’s smart brother reads and writes in Euskera, works on the radio, and is against all violence. If I had an opportunity to ask Aramburu a question, I would ask, would he have taken a different approach if he'd written about ETA during the dictatorship. Somehow I get the feeling that he wouldn’t, and moral banalities such as “terrorism is bad” make this book bland. You can’t create a brilliant political novel simply by repeating tired tropes.<br /><br />On the other hand, in the context of “Homeland”, this approach is justifiable. The setting takes place in post-Francoist Spain, and the history of ETA is the history of political degradation. When you go from the execution of Carrero Blanco to the bombings of supermarkets, it’s clear that something has gone terribly wrong. But, once again, in “Homeland” ETA doesn’t have a history and the support, the hate, the acquiescence seem to come out of nowhere.<br /><br />To Aramburu’s credit, he briefly mentions the death squads and the police terror, but in the huge panorama of personal relationships and foibles, he loses the principal question – how does the vicious circle of state and anti-state terrorism function and is there a way out? There are a few chapters, dedicated to Joxe Mari, the ETA fighter, sitting in prison and reevaluating his decisions, and these parts are good but completely lost in lengthy mundane descriptions.<br /><br />To sum up – I don’t regret reading this book, but it’s hardly comparable to Llosa’s “The Feast of the Goat” or Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie’s “Half of a Yellow Sun”. I’m still to find a decent novel to complete my TOP-3.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1847145860092354184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2151873244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2151873244" />
    <a title="Angel 一匹狼" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25600796-angel"><img alt="Angel 一匹狼" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438533669p2/25600796.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2151873244?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angel 一匹狼" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angel 一匹狼" href="/user/show/25600796-angel">Angel 一匹狼</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788490663196" href="/book/show/32042873-patria">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2151873244&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2151873244" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15068916107031315558">Nationalism, what is it good for? Absolutely nothing.<br/><br/>Yes, nationalism. That thing behind so much pain, hate and death. That sometimes is used for one's purposes but if someone else names it is a bad word. Nationalism, that is justified for our own nation but not for other's... "Patria" talks about it. In the Basque Country. "Patria" is a book that starts great, strong, with amazing characterization but then falls into bloated, over-convoluted, simplistic and ends in an unnecessary 'happy' ending</span>
  <span id="freeText15068916107031315558" style="display:none">Nationalism, what is it good for? Absolutely nothing.<br /><br />Yes, nationalism. That thing behind so much pain, hate and death. That sometimes is used for one's purposes but if someone else names it is a bad word. Nationalism, that is justified for our own nation but not for other's... "Patria" talks about it. In the Basque Country. "Patria" is a book that starts great, strong, with amazing characterization but then falls into bloated, over-convoluted, simplistic and ends in an unnecessary 'happy' ending that undoes what little of great was left in it.<br /><br />I will talk about its three biggest weaknesses in a second, but first, let's get with the introduction.<br /><br />"Patria" tells the story of two families: one is Basque, has deep roots in the community and one of the sons is a member of ETA, the paramilitary group that wanted to create an independent Basque Country in Europe. The second is Basque, has deep roots in the community and one of its members, the father, is a businessman with a logistics company that has become the target of ETA. Those two families were really really close till the moment when Txato, the businessman, failed to comply with ETA's demands and became its target. Since then, the other family stopped talking to them and the rest of the town also gave them the silent treatment. The bullying doesn't stop there, but it continues with paintings on the walls, insults and, in the end, Txato's killing by ETA. The book jumps from way before Txato's death till after ETA decides to give up its armed fighting, looking on the lives of the members from both families.<br /><br />And in the beginning it does an amazing job. It creates an atmosphere of fear and threat, and develops nicely the feeling of the everyday life that permeated the Basque Country (and Spain) after the death of Franco, with a look on the impact ETA had on its communities. Both families motivations and characters are introduced in a neat and direct style, but giving the characters time to breathe and develop. On the first hundred, two hundred pages I even thought this would be my first 5-star book this year.<br /><br />What goes wrong, then, in this story of why nationalism is bad and violence too? Well, there are different hiccups.<br /><br />First, the characters. Aramburu is too much in love with his characters. But instead of centering on Bittori, Txato, Miren... the parents of both families and maybe the son that was a member of ETA, characters that had the strongest development in the first pages, he decides to go on side quests to develop the rest of the family, and the new characters that attach to those members: the boyfriend of the gay son, the pro-Spanish husband of a daughter... It is unnecessary, it becomes convoluted and the worst crime, they are boring, it is impossible to care about them. They are superfluous.<br /><br />Second, Aramburu is also too much in love with his story and his time jumps. The story stretches and stretches... and stretches (in part because of his introducing and developing new characters). It becomes too much, too long, too boring, with too many moments of filling. And the time jumps also become too tiresome.<br /><br />Last, and maybe the worst crime, Aramburu's look on Basque Country's society is too simplistic. His message?: ETA was bad and killed, they are bad people. This was in part because of the way they understood nationalism and nation. Ok, but what about everything else that happened around it, and the reason why ETA was born, or the impact of the dictatorship on the region, or the way Spain, instead of having a truth and reconciliation commission, like South Africa, decided for amnesia: 'War, dictatorship? Here? Nothing happened.' And as the situation down the line with Catalonia showed, in Spain it was not only a problem of a violent and armed regional nationalism, but also of understanding of what makes a country, of what is multiculturalism or a nation and how if one nation (Spain) is stronger than another, the balance is difficult and the tensions abound. Related to that Aramburu also sins of simplistic understanding of what makes a person violent: Joxe Mari, unlike his brother, doesn't read, and he is young and hot-blooded, used by people that don't dirty their hands. As if smart or older people wouldn't become violent and feel justified in their actions. Old people and smart people can be, and are, violent too.<br /><br />And to add insult to injury he ends the novel with a 'happy' ending where Joxe Mari asks for forgiveness, his family understands and accepts the gay son and his mother, Miren, kind of makes up with Bittori, the wife of Txato. It is all too neat and too shallow.<br /><br />A pity. Because the beginning is really good.<br /><br />5.5/10<br /><br />(Original Castilian, cough, I mean Spanish, Version)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15068916107031315558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2843730373" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2843730373" />
    <a title="Eddie Clarke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5754022-eddie-clarke"><img alt="Eddie Clarke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310052890p2/5754022.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2843730373?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eddie Clarke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eddie Clarke" href="/user/show/5754022-eddie-clarke">Eddie Clarke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781509858033" href="/book/show/44548777-homeland">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5754022-eddie-clarke?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5754022-eddie-clarke?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5754022-eddie-clarke?shelf=reading-group">reading-group</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5754022-eddie-clarke?shelf=any-amount-of-books">any-amount-of-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2843730373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17722540321455261541">I did enjoy this with some reservations.<br/><br/>We are in very similar territory to 'Milkman' by Anna Burns - a close examination of a society and time wracked by terrorism; and the effects of that terrorism on an intimate family and closed social grouping. Both writers have personal experience, and parallels are striking (even down to the dead cat! - Aramburu's however has less freighted symbolic significance than Burns', I think).<br/><br/>Both novels employ a non-linear, mosaic-like chronological development w</span>
  <span id="freeText17722540321455261541" style="display:none">I did enjoy this with some reservations.<br /><br />We are in very similar territory to 'Milkman' by Anna Burns - a close examination of a society and time wracked by terrorism; and the effects of that terrorism on an intimate family and closed social grouping. Both writers have personal experience, and parallels are striking (even down to the dead cat! - Aramburu's however has less freighted symbolic significance than Burns', I think).<br /><br />Both novels employ a non-linear, mosaic-like chronological development which does make demands on the reader. I enjoyed Aramburu's short, sharp, apparently unconnected scenes which casually throw in seemingly tangental asides which effectively take the overall narrative forward - once I understood what he is up to. His characterisation is slow-burn as well, which make the first three hundred pages quite confusing. But the novel grows in emotional power as it progresses. Also, the second half settles down into a more classic chronologically linear tempo.<br /><br />In comparison Burns' novel takes far more narrative and stylistic risks, avoids conventionality more successfully, and from a literary point of view I would rate it as the better novel. Aramburu's overall plot, divested of his kaleidoscope structure, is actually pretty predictable: the great charm of his work is in the unpredictability of his kaleidoscope shards and how they fit together. It's this aspect, initially difficult, which ultimately kept me engrossed across 600 pages.<br /><br />I've seen some criticism here of perceived 'telenovella' aspects: I think this is slightly unfair. Unlike Burns, Aramburu is very interested in the long-term after effects of terrorism, of time creating opportunities for repentance, forgiveness and healing, and shows these aspects working out in the lives of his two families, down through two generations. Of course from a narrative perspective events will happen - Aramburu is contrasting life outside with the solitary prisoner inside wasting his life away: and these outside developments do culminate in the narrative and thematic climaxes in a satisfying way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17722540321455261541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1752945041266317659">This is one of the best books I have read in a long time. The writing, the story itself, the way the book is organized and narrated. I highly recommend this book to everyone, but especially to anyone that has ever lived in Spain or is familiar with its history. The book deals with the tensions of the terrorist group ETA and their desire to have the Basque Country secede from Spain. The way the story is told and narrated is incredibly accurate to the feelings and the culture of the region, it is </span>
  <span id="freeText1752945041266317659" style="display:none">This is one of the best books I have read in a long time. The writing, the story itself, the way the book is organized and narrated. I highly recommend this book to everyone, but especially to anyone that has ever lived in Spain or is familiar with its history. The book deals with the tensions of the terrorist group ETA and their desire to have the Basque Country secede from Spain. The way the story is told and narrated is incredibly accurate to the feelings and the culture of the region, it is a book that will most definitely make you feel and understand this issue in an incredible real way, more so if you lived through it. Could not recommend this book more. </span>

<div id="review_2818282997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2818282997" />
    <a title="Thebooktrail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25264586-thebooktrail"><img alt="Thebooktrail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455038087p2/25264586.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2818282997?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thebooktrail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thebooktrail" href="/user/show/25264586-thebooktrail">Thebooktrail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781509858033" href="/book/show/44548777-homeland">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25264586-thebooktrail?shelf=books-set-in-spain">books-set-in-spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25264586-thebooktrail?shelf=translated-fiction">translated-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2818282997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14030275705324592765"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557669382i/27494600._SX540_.jpg' width='700' height='400' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/><a href='https://www.thebooktrail.com/book-trails/homeland-fernando-aramburu/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Viist the locations in the novel </a><br/><br/><br/>This book has made an impression on me. One due to its sheer size – 581 large format pages and fairly small print, and the sheer scope of the novel itself. It aims to look at the Basque problem, the issue of terrorism of the country at large. That is one epic theme. Break it down into the involvement, opinions and experiences of two families and you start to understand the wealth of the journey the writer takes you on.<br/><br/>I almost didn’t read this as I’ve read so mu</span>
  <span id="freeText14030275705324592765" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557669382i/27494600._SX540_.jpg" width="700" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><a href="https://www.thebooktrail.com/book-trails/homeland-fernando-aramburu/" rel="nofollow noopener">Viist the locations in the novel </a><br><br><br>This book has made an impression on me. One due to its sheer size – 581 large format pages and fairly small print, and the sheer scope of the novel itself. It aims to look at the Basque problem, the issue of terrorism of the country at large. That is one epic theme. Break it down into the involvement, opinions and experiences of two families and you start to understand the wealth of the journey the writer takes you on.<br><br>I almost didn’t read this as I’ve read so much about ETA, the Basque terrorist group, terrorism in Spain and the many deaths as a result. A final University exam came to mind making my hands go all sweaty. But I did read it and it was interesting to get a view from two families who came to be on two separate sides of the political fence. That of course was a matter of life and death. Take a side, any side, and the risk was death.<br><br>What the book does well is the build up of the fear amongst the people of the Basque country and of Spain at large. The group came, grew, expanded and swept away the past without anyone noticing what was going on. The sheer scale and fear were unprecedented and this came across well in the novel.<br><br>Imagine being close friends but then not just growing apart but being forced so far apart as you can be. When someone from one family is assassinated, the member of the other family actually joins the group. It’s their stories, their reasoning, their experiences we hear. I think this is what makes for a fascinating and shocking account of the struggle. The human angle of it all is the most chilling. When one person is murdered, more lives are destroyed. Family life dies with them and this novel is a strong reminder of that.<br><br>Although this is a fictional account of the ETA years, and the setting is vague, this reads as a general yet personal account of the struggle. The author himself was born in San Sebastian in 1959 – the very year ETA was founded and so this lends extra weight to the novel for me. It’s his story and his country’s story too.<br><br>There’s some very controversial lines and ideas brought up throughout the novel but then is this not what happens during war time? We’re reading this with modern eyes so whilst certain parts jar with my understanding of the history, I can’t say it didn’t compel me to read on.<br><br>Epic in scope and style, and I have to say, for me it was over long. A shorter, more concise read would have given it a stronger impact.<br><br>Homeland (Patria in Spanish) is a novel looks at issues so relevant sadly to our modern world. And the people, the families who live them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14030275705324592765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2980547213" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2980547213" />
    <a title="Nicolas Chinardet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12332247-nicolas-chinardet"><img alt="Nicolas Chinardet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528402703p2/12332247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2980547213?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicolas Chinardet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicolas Chinardet" href="/user/show/12332247-nicolas-chinardet">Nicolas Chinardet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781509858026" href="/book/show/43418438-homeland">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2980547213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8070922955795855740">I have to admit that I found it difficult to get involved with Homeland. The fragmented nature of the narrative and timeline may have a lot to do with this. Small clusters of short, related chapters follow each other with no apparent reason for how they are cut up, or how they are ordered. This made it difficult to remember who the characters were, particularly as they all have unfamiliar Basque names. It is only really in the last third or so, when I started reading for longer periods at a time</span>
  <span id="freeText8070922955795855740" style="display:none">I have to admit that I found it difficult to get involved with Homeland. The fragmented nature of the narrative and timeline may have a lot to do with this. Small clusters of short, related chapters follow each other with no apparent reason for how they are cut up, or how they are ordered. This made it difficult to remember who the characters were, particularly as they all have unfamiliar Basque names. It is only really in the last third or so, when I started reading for longer periods at a time that I started to get more into the book. This is also where its emotional payload is located. <br /><br />As someone points out in one of the other reviews, I should perhaps also account for the fact that what the book describes it almost totally alien to me. Having grown up in France in the 80s and 90s, attacks by the ETA were not unfamiliar occurrences in the news but I have very little knowledge of the circumstances related in the book beyond those headlines. A Basque or Spanish reader would have a much strong emotional involvement, fuelled with memories and possibly direct experience. <br /><br />The quality of the translation may also have something to do with my difficulties. <br /><br />Aramburu's style is colloquial and idiosyncratic, with sudden shifts of narrative voice within sentences and intrusion of a character's internal monologue within descriptive sentences. This could be confusing but it thankfully isn't. <br /><br />Translating a book however is not an easy thing. Reading what the academic Albert MacAdam has produced is proof positive of that fact. What his shoddy effort gives us is a stilted text the language of which is often not idiomatic, gap-toothed with missing words and letters. I'm not sure why the need was felt to americanise the text, either. The success of the book and its very nature, as well as my gut feeling, tell me that it should be (probably is, in the original) much more gripping than what I experienced. <br /><br />Despite this major failing of the version I read, didn't hate the book, far from it and I am certainly glad a read it.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8070922955795855740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3176890456?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kintsugi Kitty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kintsugi Kitty" href="/user/show/109733187-kintsugi-kitty">Kintsugi Kitty</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3176890456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10139397915341321911">A very well written book with such attention to detail that you can really enter the lives of the main characters and the families that the book centres around.<br/><br/>If you are looking to understand the community and how ETA affected lives, not only of members or victims, but of those around them, then this is a must read.<br/><br/>I´ve awarded 4 stars instead of 5 as there were points, that in my opinion, that could have been improved.<br/><br/>Given the length of the book, there were unnecessary chapters/characters tha</span>
  <span id="freeText10139397915341321911" style="display:none">A very well written book with such attention to detail that you can really enter the lives of the main characters and the families that the book centres around.<br /><br />If you are looking to understand the community and how ETA affected lives, not only of members or victims, but of those around them, then this is a must read.<br /><br />I´ve awarded 4 stars instead of 5 as there were points, that in my opinion, that could have been improved.<br /><br />Given the length of the book, there were unnecessary chapters/characters that were not integral to the plot, especially the love lives of a couple of characters and their subsequent family - they felt like they had been placed in the story for author´s reasons rather than rounding out a character or development of the plot.<br /><br />There were two areas that Aramburu included in the story where he did not seem to have the mastery of the topic, which was only so obvious as the majority of the book was so well written. In fact one, he seemed so detached that it gave the impression of lack of knowledge or inexperience or even writer´s discomfort. Rather than explain them here I think it best you read the book for yourself and see what you think.<br /><br />Even with the comments above, I´d suggest reading this book if you like having an opporunity to see into how others live, to gain understanding of another culture, era and a time that very few have the bravery or talent to write about.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1999953289" />
    <a title="Alison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2417099-alison"><img alt="Alison" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1999953289?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alison" href="/user/show/2417099-alison">Alison</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1999953289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8441972854022244343">This was a very long novel, but it had to be long. It's about the twenty-odd year relationship between two Basque families. The son of one of the families becomes an assassin for ETA; the father of the other family is a victim of ETA. The novel has to be long because it is about the agonizingly slow pace of forgiveness and the agonizingly slow process of disillusionment with political violence. Aramburu does not pay much attention to place or interior monologue. The novel is dialogue, dialogue, </span>
  <span id="freeText8441972854022244343" style="display:none">This was a very long novel, but it had to be long. It's about the twenty-odd year relationship between two Basque families. The son of one of the families becomes an assassin for ETA; the father of the other family is a victim of ETA. The novel has to be long because it is about the agonizingly slow pace of forgiveness and the agonizingly slow process of disillusionment with political violence. Aramburu does not pay much attention to place or interior monologue. The novel is dialogue, dialogue, dialogue, and for a non-native speaker, this extreme colloquialism can be challenging. But the large cast of characters was totally convincing, as was the depiction of the long-term effects of violence on both families.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8441972854022244343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2795388920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5276881481161788911">The past eight day - while reading Aramburu’s book - I made a round trip in Spanish Basque Country. PRESO ETA IHESLARIAK ETXERA (bring basque prisoners back home) is still one of the most popular murals in many villages and cities. Euskadi Ta Askatasuna, was the separatist armed movement that wanted to establish an independent, socialist Basque state from 1959 to 2018. In his book ‘Vaderland’, Fernando Aramburu gives you a perfect tale of the last decennial of the dark years. Read it!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3265742932">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MQDSx2fmFmY7W3zchsQmD9tDiu+hQyo5lmFuCdsyUV7TRY/fSWopRhxH38SC6m7Jl2KZYQlDJwfUVEnbRCXlKw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lsXXMQRYszrXlTF3fV1sS3Ljl1EDYY9ZDsX6n29jPnN0gIopKtSMGvCJkm95cySNPsKE36thgmdM8N1N8HSKBg==')})
  }
//]]>

A él asiste una embarazada Amaia Salazar, la inspectora de la Policía Foral que un año atrás había resuelto los crímenes del llamado basajaun, que sembraron de terror el valle del Baztán. Amaia también había reunido las pruebas inculpatorias contra Jasón Medina, que imitando el modus operandi del basajaun había asesinado, violado y mutilado a Johana, la adolescente hija de su mujer.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/68308.Fernando_Aramburu">About Fernando Aramburu</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/68308.Fernando_Aramburu"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1348155093p3/68308.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/68308.Fernando_Aramburu">Fernando Aramburu
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
523
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer633029965971930100">Fernando Aramburu nació en San Sebastián en 1959. Es licenciado en filología hispánica por la Universidad de Zaragoza y desde 1985 reside en Alemania. Fue miembro del Grupo CLOC de Arte y Desarte. Considerado ya como uno de los narradores más destacados de su generación, es autor de tres libros de relatos: No ser no duele (1997), Los peces de la amargura (2006) y El vigilante del fiordo (2011), y </span>
  <span id="freeText633029965971930100" style="display:none">Fernando Aramburu nació en San Sebastián en 1959. Es licenciado en filología hispánica por la Universidad de Zaragoza y desde 1985 reside en Alemania. Fue miembro del Grupo CLOC de Arte y Desarte. Considerado ya como uno de los narradores más destacados de su generación, es autor de tres libros de relatos: No ser no duele (1997), Los peces de la amargura (2006) y El vigilante del fiordo (2011), y de cinco novelas: Fuegos con limón (1996), Los ojos vacíos (2000), El trompetista del Utopía (2003), Bami sin sombra (2005) y Viaje con Clara por Alemania (2010), títulos que han sido distinguidos con el Premio Ramón Gómez de la Serna 1997, el Premio Euskadi 2001, el XI Premio Mario Vargas Llosa NH, el Dulce Chacón y el Premio Real Academia Española en 2008. Ha escrito también libros para niños, como Vida de un piojo llamado Matías (2004). Con Años lentos mereció el VII Premio Tusquets Editores de Novela.</span>
  <a data-text-id="633029965971930100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
